<div id="review_690065" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/690065" />
  <a href="/user/show/52663-mer" class="left imgcol" title="Mer"><img alt="Mer" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1190790753p2/52663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/690065?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Dec 25, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/52663-mer" class="user" title="Mer">Mer</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/4248.The_Da_Vinci_Code" class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9781400079179">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">morons and troglodytes</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/52663-mer?shelf=tripeshitandgarbage" class="actionLinkLite">tripeshitandgarbage</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer690065" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3601188374959359463">PLEASE do NOT recommend The Da Vinci Code to me because you think it's brilliant. Please do not try to explain to me that it is a "really interesting and eye-opening book." Just don't. Please. <br><br>I've read Iain Pear, I heart Foucault's Pendulum, Dashiell Hammett is my hero, Alan Moore is My Absolute Favorite, I listen to Coil on a fairly regular basis, and cloak n' dagger secret society/Priory of Sion/Knights of Templar-tinged num nums make me a very happy girl... but if you truly believe that Brow</span>
  <span id="freeText3601188374959359463" style="display:none">PLEASE do NOT recommend The Da Vinci Code to me because you think it's brilliant. Please do not try to explain to me that it is a "really interesting and eye-opening book." Just don't. Please. <br><br>I've read Iain Pear, I heart Foucault's Pendulum, Dashiell Hammett is my hero, Alan Moore is My Absolute Favorite, I listen to Coil on a fairly regular basis, and cloak n' dagger secret society/Priory of Sion/Knights of Templar-tinged num nums make me a very happy girl... but if you truly believe that Brown's stupid airport thriller has ANY right whatsoever to be placed in the same category with Michael "Wooden Dildo Dialogue" Crichton, let alone Umberto Eco, kindly keep this opinion very far away from me, or the ensuing conversation we have will not be constructive or polite in any way.<br><br>I loathe Dan Brown. I resent him for spoon-feeding the masses pseudo-intellectual "Holy Blood, Holy Grail" D-grade thriller shite under a pretense of real sophistication, and getting orally serviced by The New York Times for his effort.<br><br>I'd heard that the novel was meticulously researched and contained some really interesting and controversial assessments of religious zealotry. Um, not really? Well, not by my Merovingian standards, anyway. :D<br><br>Let's put it this way. If Dan Brown was teaching an Insurgent Christian Symbolism in Art and Literature 101 class at my local community college, I'd definitely have a different opinion about him. <br><br>But NO. Dan Brown is not a professor of anything but pap. He is a barely competent thriller writer who wrote an AWFUL book that I could not bear to finish because I felt my IQ plummeting a little further with every "Let's Go to Paris! Guidebook" description and blowhard authorial essay. Oh, don't even get me started about those cute soliloquies the main characters are so fond of delivering, ever so calmly, often while cops n' bovvers are chasing them.<br><br>The characters are weakly drawn. The dialogue is excruciating. The research is shoddy and self-serving at best. The plot, no matter how open-minded you are, is beyond ludicrous. It's laughable enough to be incorporated into the next Indiana Jones movie. That'd be sweet, dude.<br><br>What really irks me are Dan Brown's sanctimonious interviews, wherein he shows off all of his priceless antiques while expressing his abiding convictions that the American public needs a "deeper appreciation" of art and history and culture. What a shallow, self-aggrandizing hypocrite. I'm all for fictional subversion of the dominant Catholic paradigm, but only if the subverter knows what the hell they're talking about. Brown DOESN'T. He's all "la la la, connect the dots" but the picture he comes up with is awkward and unconvincing.<br><br>The DaVinci Choad is a dead easy, nay, downright lazy read, and yet droves of people are patting themselves on the back for having read and *gasp* actually understood it. Like this is some spectacular achievement? WHY? What, because the slipcover describes it as "erudite"? Are you fucking kidding me?<br><br>Don't believe the hype, kids. You are profoundly more intelligent than this holiday page-turner gives you credit for.<br><br>If you really, honestly, just plain liked the book, that's cool I guess. Maybe you also prefer Anne Geddes to Alfred Stieglitz, Kenny G to Sidney Bechet, John Tesh to Igor Stravinsky. Your prerogative. Just.... please don't try to tell me that this is "fascinating" or "meaningful literature". Frickin' read The Club Dumas or something. Then we'll talk, and I won't want to shoot myself in the face.<br><br>Alright, glad I purged that poison from my system. Carry on.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3601188374959359463" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/62391-ryan" class="user" title="ryan">ryan</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5828868495785965431">most of us have heard of this controverisal book. it takes an open minded person to read this and to remember it is just fiction. but it brings up a lot of important questions about the Christian church, and the loss of paganism and the respect of the Goddess or the Woman. <br><br>I don't care if I am the only one who likes this book. it is my own truth, and i will think what i want to think. Dan Brown didn't LEAD me or anyone else. he OPENED our minds. simply and importantly...he was just a catalyst fo</span>
  <span id="freeText5828868495785965431" style="display:none">most of us have heard of this controverisal book. it takes an open minded person to read this and to remember it is just fiction. but it brings up a lot of important questions about the Christian church, and the loss of paganism and the respect of the Goddess or the Woman. <br><br>I don't care if I am the only one who likes this book. it is my own truth, and i will think what i want to think. Dan Brown didn't LEAD me or anyone else. he OPENED our minds. simply and importantly...he was just a catalyst for different thinking. that is a good thing...poorly written or not.<br><br>if you finish the book you will notice that Dan Brown even makes it clear to readers through his characters words, that he doesn't want to destroy christianity because it has done so much good for so many people, and if it works for them, let's let them continue to do what works for them. but find your own path. <br><br>if you were or are a Christian ask yourself about the topics in this book. They are so eye opening. Jesus having a baby? totally possible...never thought of it before. never thought of it. is it true? who knows. Things like this are happening all the time today...Weapons of Mass destruction in Iraq? sound familiar? Maybe the church repressed information LIKE this because it was a threat to the church. totally possible. The catholic church creating the biblical canon with a political agenda to wipe out paganism? actually this seems to be a fact. women being oppressed due to the fear of religous zealot men in power losing their power...never looked at it that way. but this seems to be a fact too. is it helpful in broadening my perspective of the fact that christianity is just a religion made by fallible people. it sure is. does it open my mind to other faiths like paganism, judiasm, islam, bhuddism, and want to take the truths from all of them, and then THINK FOR MYSELF and figure out my own truth. it sure does...and that is what this book has probably done for many other people. why do you think Dan Brown's book was on the bestseller list for so long...and became a movie...obviously it was doing some good.</span>

<div id="review_27337374" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/27337374" />
  <a href="/user/show/1137738-ethan" class="left imgcol" title="Ethan"><img alt="Ethan" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1265525727p2/1137738.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/27337374?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Jul 15, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1137738-ethan" class="user" title="Ethan">Ethan</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_3" title="liked it">3 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/4248.The_Da_Vinci_Code" class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9781400079179">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer27337374" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14080412827069042273">Four stars for <strong>pure entertainment value</strong>.<br><br>However, Dave Barry's review gets five stars:<br><br><br>`The Da Vinci Code,' cracked<br>by Dave Barry<br><br>I have written a blockbuster novel. My inspiration was The DaVinci Code by Dan Brown, which has sold 253 trillion copies in hardcover because it's such a compelling page-turner. NOBODY can put this book down:<br><br>MOTHER ON BEACH: Help! My child is being attacked by a shark!<br><br>LIFEGUARD (looking up from The DaVinci Code: Not now! I just got to page 243, where it turns out that one</span>
  <span id="freeText14080412827069042273" style="display:none">Four stars for <strong>pure entertainment value</strong>.<br><br>However, Dave Barry's review gets five stars:<br><br><br>`The Da Vinci Code,' cracked<br>by Dave Barry<br><br>I have written a blockbuster novel. My inspiration was The DaVinci Code by Dan Brown, which has sold 253 trillion copies in hardcover because it's such a compelling page-turner. NOBODY can put this book down:<br><br>MOTHER ON BEACH: Help! My child is being attacked by a shark!<br><br>LIFEGUARD (looking up from The DaVinci Code: Not now! I just got to page 243, where it turns out that one of the men depicted in ''The Last Supper'' is actually a woman!<br><br>MOTHER: I know! Isn't that incredible? And it turns out that she's . . .<br><br>SHARK (spitting out the child): Don't give it away! I'm only on page 187!<br><br>The key to The DaVinci Code is that it's filled with startling plot twists, and almost every chapter ends with a ''cliffhanger,'' so you have to keep reading to see what will happen. Using this formula, I wrote the following blockbuster novel, titled The Constitution Conundrum. It's fairly short now, but when I get a huge publishing contract, I'll flesh it out to 100,000 words by adding sentences.<br><br>CHAPTER ONE: Handsome yet unmarried historian Hugh Heckman stood in the National Archives Building in Washington, D.C., squinting through the bulletproof glass at the U.S. Constitution. Suddenly, he made an amazing discovery.<br><br>''My God!'' he said, out loud. ``This is incredible! Soon I will say what it is.''<br><br>CHAPTER TWO: ''What is it?'' said a woman Heckman had never seen before who happened to be standing next to him. She was extremely beautiful, but wore glasses as a sign of intelligence.<br><br>''My name is Desiree Legume,'' she said.<br><br>Heckman felt he could trust her.<br><br>''Look at this!'' he said, pointing to the Constitution.<br><br>''My God, that's incredible!'' said Desiree. ``It's going to be very surprising when we finally reveal what we're talking about!''<br><br>CHAPTER THREE: ''Yes,'' said Hugh, ``incredible as it seems, there are extra words written in the margin of the U.S. Constitution, and nobody ever noticed them until now! They appear to be in some kind of code.''<br><br>''Let me look,'' said Desiree. ``In addition to being gorgeous, I am a trained codebreaker. Oh my God!''<br><br>''What is it?'' asked Hugh in an excited yet concerned tone of voice. ''The message,'' said Desiree, ``is . . . ''<br><br>But just then, the chapter ended.<br><br>CHAPTER FOUR: ''It's a fiendishly clever code,'' explained Desiree. 'As you can see, the words say: `White House White House Bo Bite House, Banana Fana Fo Fite House, Fe Fi Mo Mite House, White House.' ''<br><br>''Yes,'' said Hugh, frowning in bafflement. ``But what can it possibly mean?''<br><br>''If I am correct,'' said Desiree, ``it is referring to . . . the White House!''<br><br>''My God!'' said Hugh. ``That's where the president lives! Do you think . . . ''<br><br>''Do I think what?'' said Desiree.<br><br>''I don't know,'' said Hugh. ``But we're about to find out.''<br><br>CHAPTER FIVE: Hugh and Desiree crouched in some bushes next to the Oval Office.<br><br>''We'd better hurry up and solve this mystery,'' remarked Desiree anxiously. ''It's only a matter of time before somebody notices that the Constitution is missing.'' She had slipped it into her purse at the National Archives while the guard wasn't looking.<br><br>''The answer must be here somewhere,'' said Hugh, studying the ancient document, which was brown from age and the fact that he had spilled Diet Peach Snapple on it.<br><br>''Wait a minute!'' he said. ``I've got it!''<br><br>''What?'' said Desiree, her breasts heaving into view.<br><br>''The answer!'' said Hugh. ``It's . . .<br><br>But just then, shots rang out.<br><br>CHAPTER SIX: ''That was close!'' remarked Desiree. ``Fortunately, those shots had nothing to do with the plot of this book.''<br><br>''Yes,'' said Hugh. ``Anyway, as I was saying, the answer is to hold the Constitution up so that it is aligned with the White House and the Washington Monument. . . . There, do you see what I mean?''<br><br>''My God!'' said Desiree, seeing what he meant. ``It's . . . ''<br><br>''Hold it right there,'' said the president of the United States.<br><br>CHAPTER SEVEN: '' . . . and so you see,'' concluded the president, ``you two uncovered a shocking and fascinating secret that, if it should ever get out, could change the course of history.''<br><br>''Mr. President,'' said Desiree, ``thank you for that riveting and satisfying explanation, which will be fleshed out into much greater detail once there is a publishing contract.''<br><br>''Also,'' noted Hugh, ``we may use some beverage other than Snapple, depending on what kind of product-placement deals can be worked out.''<br><br>''Good,'' said the president. ``Now can I have the Constitution back?''<br><br>They all enjoyed a hearty laugh, for they knew that the movie rights were also available...</span>

<div id="review_3181304" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3181304" />
  <a href="/user/show/198340-jim" class="left imgcol" title="Jim"><img alt="Jim" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1366461973p2/198340.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/3181304?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Jul 17, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/198340-jim" class="user" title="Jim">Jim</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>



        

    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3181304" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2590082732492806328">This is a pretty formulaic page turner, a fun quick read. Written at about the level of the average Nancy Drew mystery, it is best appreciated at that level. As far as the content, there are howlers on virtually every page (starting with the hero who looks like "Harrison Ford in Harris tweed" and is a "Professor of Religious Symbology at Harvard" -- good work if you can find it). You have to ignore very pulpy, cheesy writing to enjoy this romantic thriller.<br><br>Intended as a book that a dedicated rea</span>
  <span id="freeText2590082732492806328" style="display:none">This is a pretty formulaic page turner, a fun quick read. Written at about the level of the average Nancy Drew mystery, it is best appreciated at that level. As far as the content, there are howlers on virtually every page (starting with the hero who looks like "Harrison Ford in Harris tweed" and is a "Professor of Religious Symbology at Harvard" -- good work if you can find it). You have to ignore very pulpy, cheesy writing to enjoy this romantic thriller.<br><br>Intended as a book that a dedicated reader could finish in a day, or something you take to the beach and casually finish in a weekend, <em>The Da Vinci Code</em> makes for a reasonable airline novel, so much so that it is often a bit clunky in its desire to ensure that no intellectual effort on the reader's part will be required. Here's a recurring example in this novel: a bit of unfamiliar terminology, say "crux gemmata" (jeweled cross) will will be explained on page N, then on page N+1, a character will finger his jeweled cross and explain, "Oh, yes -- this is a crux gemmata." I've read dinner menus that were more demanding on the reader. My wife and I both read about a third of it in a day, sharing the same copy, and that's a full work day plus taking care of kids, bedtime, etc. That's also a kind of virtue, I guess -- it's fast and peppy.<br><br>As far as history goes, Dan Brown apparently thinks that "most historians" give credence to the hoary forgeries and frauds promoted in sensationalist best-sellers like <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Holy%20Blood,%20Holy%20Grail" title="Holy Blood, Holy Grail">Holy Blood, Holy Grail</a>. This author gets the best of both worlds: simultaneously claiming that "it's just fiction," while introducing the novel with claims that the historical record contained within is "fact." That claim is ridiculous. To pluck a random example, he spends some time talking about the Council of Nicaea, and incorrectly summarizes it as the origin of the doctrine of Christ's divinity by Constantine. He ignores the Arian controversy out of which it arose, which is like trying to explain the Treaty of Versailles without mentioning World War I. He ignores the documented fact, agreed upon even by the cheerleaders of the gnostics that he is sympathetic to, that the earliest gnostic doctrines held that Christ was *purely* God, and not really man -- the very reverse of the doctrine that serves as the linchpin of his novel's intellectual base (such as it is). This is a bad novel for weak or misinformed Christians, but anyone familiar with history should spot the train wreck of Brown's ideas a mile off.<br><br>Oh yes, and in Brown's world, Opus Dei has shadowy assassin "monks" (in real life, Opus Dei is not a monastic order -- there are no Opus Dei monks, let alone trained assassins), and the Catholic Church has been promulgating known lies as its central dogmas, promotes violence throughout the world, and has been retarding the progress of science and knowledge for 2 millennia. Brown leaves the reader with the impression that this, too, is a matter of settled historical record. Oh, but then again, it's just fiction. Except when it's not.<br><br>In general, if you're looking for a heady thriller wrapped around Christian arcana, I'd recommend Umberto Eco's excellent <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=The%20Name%20of%20the%20Rose" title="The Name of the Rose">The Name of the Rose</a>, not this dumbed down, by-the-numbers novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2590082732492806328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_31092669" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/31092669" />
  <a href="/user/show/1413439-stephen" class="left imgcol" title="Stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1311616811p2/1413439.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/31092669?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Aug 22, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1413439-stephen" class="user" title="Stephen">Stephen</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_3" title="liked it">3 of 5 stars</a>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=conspiracies-and-weird-science" class="actionLinkLite">conspiracies-and-weird-science</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=2000-2005" class="actionLinkLite">2000-2005</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=mystery" class="actionLinkLite">mystery</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=thriller" class="actionLinkLite">thriller</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=religion-spirituality" class="actionLinkLite">religion-spirituality</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=audiobook" class="actionLinkLite">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer31092669" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2316004511868318179"><strong>OKAY PEOPLE</strong>â¦someone let me in on the gag because between the cries of <em>"Greatest Book of Greaty Greatness EVER"</em> and the screams of <em>"Lamest Load of Lamey Lameness EVER"</em>, my itty bitty brain is left veryâ¦  <a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Confused%20and%20surprised/?action=view&amp;current=tilda-swinton-dazed-and-confused-may-2010-second-cover-1-1-1.jpg" rel="nofollow">
  <img src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380315862/689217.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="escapedImg"></a><br>So <strong>post Hype-a-ganza</strong>, I finally got around to reading this popular, polarizing, pop culture icon and thought it wasâ¦.drum rollâ¦â¦â¦â¦â¦â¦â¦â¦FINE(sigh). It was a solid read with a slight lean towards the âehâ side of MEH and few moments of genuine âthatâs neat.â I donât see all the love and I donât see</span>
  <span id="freeText2316004511868318179" style="display:none"><strong>OKAY PEOPLE</strong>â¦someone let me in on the gag because between the cries of <em>"Greatest Book of Greaty Greatness EVER"</em> and the screams of <em>"Lamest Load of Lamey Lameness EVER"</em>, my itty bitty brain is left veryâ¦  <a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Confused%20and%20surprised/?action=view&amp;current=tilda-swinton-dazed-and-confused-may-2010-second-cover-1-1-1.jpg" rel="nofollow">
  <img src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380315862/689217.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="escapedImg"></a><br>So <strong>post Hype-a-ganza</strong>, I finally got around to reading this popular, polarizing, pop culture icon and thought it wasâ¦.drum rollâ¦â¦â¦â¦â¦â¦â¦â¦FINE(sigh). It was a solid read with a slight lean towards the âehâ side of MEH and few moments of genuine âthatâs neat.â I donât see all the love and I donât see all the rage. Other than the obvious religious flavor of the content, it reminded me of your typical page-turning, popcorn beach read and I thought it accomplished its goal in decent, if unremarkable, fashion. <br><br>Now I have a strict <em>âdonât ask, donât tell, donât mock, donât preachâ</em> approach when it comes to religiousness so I am going to ignore the <strong>bird-flipping Vatican bash</strong> aspects of the story, though I can certainly see people on both sides of the fence having âepic rah rahâ or âepic failâ reactions and I respect that. For me, it didnât move my needle much in either direction beyond my fondness for the âbig hidden history mysteryâ which is something I generally really enjoy. <br><br>The plot of this one has been talked to death and beyond so rather than adding one more jelly bean to the jar, I thought I would just run down a few likes and dislikes about the story and leave it at that. <br><br><strong>
  <u>TURN ONS</u>
</strong><br><br>1. <u>Conspiracy theories</u>: are just fully fun and I am a major sucker for plots concerning âshadowâ histories and secret people doing secret things behind secret doors for reasons that are SHHHHHHH. I love a good conspiracy. Find me a rumor involving <em> Kim Kardashian</em> being a <em>Bilderberger</em> and using a secret banking pipeline running from <em>Area 51</em> through <em>Microsoft</em> to the <em>Saudi Royal Family</em> and laundering vast monies to be used to coordinate the sale of <em>Boise, Idaho</em> to a <em>mysterious consortium</em> headed by <em> Jay Z and Justin Bieber</em> who will then turn the city into a giant <em>quasi-government facility</em> used for the testing of alien âcloningâ technologyâ¦â¦â¦â¦.and I am glued to my seat and ONE HAPPY FELLA. <br><br>2. <u>Knights Templar</u>: As much as I love conspiracies in general, when you throw the Knights Templar into the mix, itâs gonna perk me up better than a latte enema. I am always in favor of having them show up as a lynch pin to any massive global plot. The Knights Templar are like caramel on ice cream and just make a good conspiracy better. I had a lot of fun with the rehash of the Templarâs place in the center of EVERYTHING.  <br><br>3. <u>Symbology, Da Vinci and the Holy Grail <strong>(the IDEA)</strong></u>: I thought the major plot components themselves were interesting and I enjoyed following the hidden clues, messages, riddles and the tie in to all of the famous historical artifacts. It was fun. I also liked the âhistorical significanceâ of the search (i.e., the âbig revealâ) and the implications to the world if revealed.      <br><br><strong>
  <u>TURN OFFS</u>
</strong><br><br>1. <u>Symbology, Da Vinci and the Holy Grail <strong>(the EXECUTION)</strong></u>: As much as I enjoyed the plot concept, the execution of the story was often frustrating and occasionally insulting. Iâm not talking about the clunky, âserviceable at bestâ prose as thatâs gotten enough play without my squirting lighter fluid on the bonfire. My issue is more with Dan feeling the need to âspoon feedâ me details about his âoh so clever plotâ so that my economy-sized brain could grasp it.    <br><a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Animals/?action=view&amp;current=5128104164_ConfusedCat_answer_2_xlarge.jpg" rel="nofollow">
  <img src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380315862/689218.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="escapedImg"></a><br>For example, there would be a ârevealâ that I thought was interestingâ¦.and then Dan would exhaust me with explaining EXACTLY what that meant and EXACTLY what the implications were and make sure I knew EXACTLY what he had told me. I get it Mr. Brown, heard you the first time. <br><br>2. <u>THRILLer killing amounts of PLOD</u>: For a page turning, actiony thriller, there was just too much sideways movement of the plot and some really unnecessary amounts of plod to the narrative. Part of this has to do with the excessive âhand holdingâ Dan does with his audience mentioned above. However, there are also WAY too much time spent slowing down to take a look around and where we are and where weâve been. I started getting the impression that Brown was trying to hit a particular page count for the book and didnât have anything but filler to loan the pages with.  This is never a good thing for this kind of story. <br><br>3. <u>The End</u>: Not a big fan of the final resolution of the story and I found it very <strong>un climaxy</strong> and a bit of a let down. Once we have the big reveal, very little new information ever really got added to the picture and I felt like my curiosity should have been stroked a few more times than it was in the home stretch. This lack of satisfying climax left me with a serious case of <strong>âblue brain.â</strong> <br><br>Still, overall, this was a good, serviceable mystery-thriller that seems tailor-made for a warm afternoon on the sand. It isnât great literature, or even good literature, but it is a good thriller, a good concept and, for the most part, fun. It seems to accomplish pretty much exactly what it set out to do.     <br><br>2.5 to 3.0 stars.<br></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13675105199775327379">Stephan, don't pay to much attention to others reviews. Lots of these people are self-appointed pedantic little erudites extolling their fictitious pe</span>
  <span id="freeText13675105199775327379" style="display:none">Stephan, don't pay to much attention to others reviews. Lots of these people are self-appointed pedantic little erudites extolling their fictitious pedigree of literary prowess because this is the only place anyone might read what they have to say. <br>If a piece of literature doesn't entertain, teach or more importantly, puts in type-face the words you've been looking to find expression, it's fairly useless.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13675105199775327379" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
    <br/>

    <span class="greyText">
      27. Januar, 20:30 Uhr
    </span>

<div id="review_1194579" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1194579" />
  <a href="/user/show/84023-keely" class="left imgcol" title="Keely"><img alt="Keely" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1274333636p2/84023.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1194579?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">May 26, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/84023-keely" class="user" title="Keely">Keely</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/85266.The_Da_Vinci_Code" class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385504201">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/84023-keely?shelf=contemporary-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">contemporary-fiction</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/84023-keely?shelf=novel" class="actionLinkLite">novel</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/84023-keely?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/84023-keely?shelf=america" class="actionLinkLite">america</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1194579" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12695377111873992888">A thriller devoid of pacing or exciting language. A mystery devoid of clues, foreshadowing, or facts. A tell-all of half-truths based upon a forged document written by a schizophrenic conman. A character-driven modern novel devoid of character. The second draft of Angels and Demons. Page-turning action thanks to the literary equivalent of pulling out at the moment of orgasm. A spiritual awakening built on new-age conspiracy theory. This book is many things, and none of them good, new, or interes</span>
  <span id="freeText12695377111873992888" style="display:none">A thriller devoid of pacing or exciting language. A mystery devoid of clues, foreshadowing, or facts. A tell-all of half-truths based upon a forged document written by a schizophrenic conman. A character-driven modern novel devoid of character. The second draft of Angels and Demons. Page-turning action thanks to the literary equivalent of pulling out at the moment of orgasm. A spiritual awakening built on new-age conspiracy theory. This book is many things, and none of them good, new, or interesting. However, it is an excellent litmus test for idealistic delusion.<br><br>Upon the first reading, I must admit I found it a bit interesting, but then I turned the final page, and there was no bibliography. No explanation of how the author became familiar with all the concepts he claimed to 'faithfully portray'. He wrote this book and pretended it was a history book, and then refused to support it in any way. And any history you can't check up on is a bad one. <br><br>He's no better than James Frey. In fact, he may be worse, since I know people who base their religious beliefs on this book, whereas Frey's only crime was wishing he was Scarface. And really, what macho thirtysomething male doesn't?<br><br>Brown had good reasons for hiding his sources: they were forged by con-man Pierre Plantard and snuck into the Bibliotheque National in Paris back in the seventies. And it's not like Plantard got away with it, either--the whole 'Priory of Sion' thing was debunked thirty years before this book was even written.<br><br>The artistic 'iconography' that figures heavily into the mystery is also completely made-up, and was declared ludicrous by an art history professor of my acquaintance. There are a lot of well-known symbols and allusions in classic art, but none of them resemble Brown's claims. The whole hinge on which the plot turns--the notion that an inverted triangle is automatically symbolic of women--makes about as much sense as declaring that the use of the swastika by 3rd century, BC Buddhists was proof that they were fascists.<br><br>The rest of Brown's book is filled with the sort of cliched religious conspiracies you get from your first year as a theology student. Not only that, but these conspiracies were already explored by better writers in <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17841.Foucault_s_Pendulum">'Foucault's Pendulum'</a> and the <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53504572">'Illuminatus! Trilogy'</a>.<br><br>Well, I've already done more legitimate historical research on this review than Brown did in his whole book, so I guess I'll call it a day.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12695377111873992888" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

Ø§ÙØ­ÙØ§Ø¦Ù Ø§ÙØªÙ ÙØªØ¨ÙØ§ ÙØ§ÙØ¬Ø¯ÙÙ Ø­ÙÙ Ø°ÙÙ Ø§ÙÙÙØ¶ÙØ¹<br><blockquote>""These books can't possibly compete with centuries of established history, especially when that history is endorsed by the ultimate bestseller of all time."<br>Faukman's eyes went wide. "Don't tell me <u>Harry Potter</u> is actually about the Holy Grail."<br>"I was referring to the <u>Bible</u>."</blockquote>

<div id="review_3783418" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3783418" />
  <a href="/user/show/230734-maura" class="left imgcol" title="Maura"><img alt="Maura" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1186507152p2/230734.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/3783418?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Jul 30, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/230734-maura" class="user" title="Maura">Maura</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">someone interested in a completely mindless beach read</span></div>

        

    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3783418" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15121248491060024489">I've finally started reading that ever so controversial best-seller by Dan Brown.  Actually, not reading it, listening to it while driving around Lansing, MI.  This book seems to have changed the minds of many Catholics (my grandfather included) and Protestants alike.  Granted, there have long been rumors of secret societies and organizations within the Roman Catholic Church, and historical cover-ups are rampant throughout civilization.  <br><br>HOWEVER,<br><br>The book is crap.  It's not at all well written.  Brown</span>
  <span id="freeText15121248491060024489" style="display:none">I've finally started reading that ever so controversial best-seller by Dan Brown.  Actually, not reading it, listening to it while driving around Lansing, MI.  This book seems to have changed the minds of many Catholics (my grandfather included) and Protestants alike.  Granted, there have long been rumors of secret societies and organizations within the Roman Catholic Church, and historical cover-ups are rampant throughout civilization.  <br><br>HOWEVER,<br><br>The book is crap.  It's not at all well written.  Brown seems to feel that in order to impress the mystery of the supposed Holy Grail conspiracy upon his readers, he must be repetitive and condescending.  It almost seems that the whole purpose of the book is to tell the world how much Brown knows about obscure art history and symbology, and that he is willing to explain it to the teeming masses of uniformed Christendom.  His constant use of cliff-hanger chapter endings (almost every chapter) makes the novel read like it was originally intended as a serial publication.  Much of Brown's story hinges upon the loss of the Sacred Feminine, and yet his main female character (a cryptologist for the French police) is constantly having to be led clue by clue to obvious conclusions by her quicker, more worldly, male counterparts.<br><br>I might have put some stock into Brown's "history," he writes with conviction, if not much style.  I may even have looked into some of his sources on my own.  Today, though, Brown completely lost any stock I would have put into his actual knowledge.  He referred, multiple times, to Jesus Christ as the Immaculate Conception.  As every half-informed Catholic knows, <strong>Mary</strong> was the Immaculate Conception (conceived without sin), Jesus was the <em>Miraculous</em> Conception (conceived by the power of the Holy Spirit).<br><br>How this novel came to be as popular as it is, I can understand.  Everyone today is dying to get to the big TRUTH, something which can never be done in religion.  Faith is by definition something that is unsubstantiated, we must just believe.  What I can't understand is how people can believe this absolute drivel.  <br></span>
  <a data-text-id="15121248491060024489" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_3" title="liked it">3 of 5 stars</a>

  <span id="freeText5805659509818825178" style="display:none"><strong>Book Circle Reads 11</strong><br><br>Rating: 3 stars of five <br><br><strong>The Publisher Says</strong>: An ingenious code hidden in the works of Leonardo da Vinci. A desperate race through the cathedrals and castles of Europe. An astonishing truth concealed for centuries . . . unveiled at last.<br><br>While in Paris, Harvard symbologist Robert Langdon is awakened by a phone call in the dead of the night. The elderly curator of the Louvre has been murdered inside the museum, his body covered in baffling symbols. As Langdon and gifted French cryptologist Sophie Neveu sort through the bizarre riddles, they are stunned to discover a trail of clues hidden in the works of Leonardo da Vinciâclues visible for all to see and yet ingeniously disguised by the painter.<br><br>Even more startling, the late curator was involved in the Priory of Sionâa secret society whose members included Sir Isaac Newton, Victor Hugo, and Da Vinciâand he guarded a breathtaking historical secret. Unless Langdon and Neveu can decipher the labyrinthine puzzleâwhile avoiding the faceless adversary who shadows their every moveâthe explosive, ancient truth will be lost forever.  <br><br><strong>My Review</strong>:  Not one word. I mean it. Not ONE WORD of criticism for this book's three-star rating. It is not Literature, it is not even particularly well-written farb, but it is undeniably a page-turning rip-snorting adventure story that pokes fun at christian religion. Therefore it is A-Okay with me.<br><br>Snobs: It's not about you. It's about normal people getting their entertainment from a book for once, instead of a TV or a gaming console. Why are you bitching? Who said you had to read it?<br><br>Lovers: It's not about how much you love it. I didn't love it. I read the whole thing in a sitting and I wasn't about to get up until it was done, and that's saying a lot for someone whose life list of books read includes the snooty people's snootiest books. So yeah, three-star review is a huge vote of confidence from this source.<br><br>Religious christians: What in the hell are you doing reading my reviews?! Are you daft? I won't be saying anything nice about your imaginary friend any time soon. Pass on!<br><br>Environmentalists: Yes, the entirety of Siberia was deforested to print the book in its zillions. I feel bad about that too. Tell you what: Get out there and make hemp paper (better for the environment, plus a smokeable side product!) on a commercial scale. Books will go down in price, forests will be saved, and the mellow quotient of the world will go up. Win-win-win!<br><br>Normal people: You've all read the book by now, right? If not, go to a used bookstore (Brown's rich enough) and pick a few up. It's a lot of fun.<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/deed.en_US" rel="nofollow">

<div id="review_16111782" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16111782" />
  <a href="/user/show/107301-robert" class="left imgcol" title="Robert"><img alt="Robert" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1180568811p2/107301.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/16111782?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Aug 08, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/107301-robert" class="user" title="Robert">Robert</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/4248.The_Da_Vinci_Code" class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9781400079179">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/107301-robert?shelf=female-divinity" class="actionLinkLite">female-divinity</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer16111782" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8826981479366782809">For the most part, it seems that people either passionately love this book or they passionately hate it. I happen to be one of the former. For my part, I don't see the book so much as an indictment of the Catholic Church in particular but of religious extremism and religion interfering in political process in general. The unwarranted political control granted to extreme religious organizations like the CBN is an issue that we will be forced to address one way or the other. To my eye, our politic</span>
  <span id="freeText8826981479366782809" style="display:none">For the most part, it seems that people either passionately love this book or they passionately hate it. I happen to be one of the former. For my part, I don't see the book so much as an indictment of the Catholic Church in particular but of religious extremism and religion interfering in political process in general. The unwarranted political control granted to extreme religious organizations like the CBN is an issue that we will be forced to address one way or the other. To my eye, our political process has been poisoned by it and the danger of theocracy is quite real. Furthermore, Brown's indictment of the Church for removing or suppressing feminine divinity figures is justified and needs a much closer look. Women do not have enough of a role in religion, religious practice, heroic myths, and creation myths, nor are they portrayed as divinity figures enough. In short, our religious systems and institutions lack balance and have a bias to suppress issues, stories, and roles that empower women to live as equals to men. Finally, Brown wrote his story simplistically, in my view, to spread his tale to as broad an audience as possible. Though it is not as pristine a narrative as, say, Umberto Eco, the message it conveys is one that needs to be heard. More obscure books on the matter are not as accessible as Da Vinci Code and if someone were to write an accessible book of genius on this subject, I would give him/her all due praise. In the meantime, Dan Brown is telling a story that needs to be told. It is one that has been kept quiet and in the dark for far too long.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8826981479366782809" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_18675890" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18675890" />
  <a href="/user/show/1008029-cj" class="left imgcol" title="CJ"><img alt="CJ" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1222819434p2/1008029.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/18675890?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Jun 01, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1008029-cj" class="user" title="CJ">CJ</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people who are gullible</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/1008029-cj?shelf=mystery" class="actionLinkLite">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18675890" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7119893251087795734">Caveat Academics!!!<br>I won't belabor the obvious, as it's been done quite well by other reviewers, but I just couldn't stand not to add my own "hear hear!" to the fray. If you're going to create a character who is an expert, PLEASE PLEASE PLEASE make sure you check your facts! Whoever edited this drivel ought to be sewn in a sack with a rabid raccoon and flung into Lake Michigan.<br><br>And just as a matter of good taste - your expert should not be an expert in everything under the sun. That's one of the</span>
  <span id="freeText7119893251087795734" style="display:none">Caveat Academics!!!<br>I won't belabor the obvious, as it's been done quite well by other reviewers, but I just couldn't stand not to add my own "hear hear!" to the fray. If you're going to create a character who is an expert, PLEASE PLEASE PLEASE make sure you check your facts! Whoever edited this drivel ought to be sewn in a sack with a rabid raccoon and flung into Lake Michigan.<br><br>And just as a matter of good taste - your expert should not be an expert in everything under the sun. That's one of the hallmarks of poor writing.<br><br>Even if I were not a practicing pagan, I would find it stretching credibility that every single item the characters run across is a symbol of goddess worship. Five pointed star? Goddess worship. Chalice? Goddess worship. Porcelain toilet bowl? Goddess worship. Pilot ball point pen? Goddess worship. You get the general idea. Not only is every item part of the mythology of the divine feminine, but every number is also part of the divine feminine. Hello? Is a cigar NEVER just a cigar? <br><br>And some of the claims of symbolism are just plain wrong, as the editor would have found out if he'd bothered to do some fact checking. Remember those military chevrons that, because of the way they were pointed, represented the female divine and those poor slobs of soldiers had been running around all these countless centuries with goddess symbols flaunted on their uniforms without knowing it? The only problem with that premise is that the chevrons facing in their current direction is relatively recent - according to my military historian husband, they faced the OPPOSITE direction for quite some time before being reversed (for what reason, I have no idea...unless the generals all got together and decided they didn't have quite enough goddess symbols on their uniforms and needed it fixed post haste).<br><br>My theology professor ended up traveling around the country giving talks about this book to thousands of interested people. He loves the book if only because he's now giving pretty much the same information that he used to give to dozing freshman and sophomores to packed theaters of interested listeners. He tells a story about being somewhere in southern Ohio and making a joking remark about the celice being something that all Catholics wore and how now the secret was out, and there was a lady in the back row who elbowed her husband and said "See? I told you so!" The increased interest in history is about the only positive thing that's come out of this book. Honestly, you don't need to make anything up about the Catholic church to point out that it's been the source of some horrible things.<br><br>I could go on about the poor research and editing in this book, but others have done a pretty thorough job of finding the problems with it.<br><br>If you want a decent page turner, go for it. If you want something well researched and accurate, give this one a big ol' pass.<br><br><br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="7119893251087795734" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

I did get through the whole thing. The dialog is excruciating. The research is shoddy and self-serving at best. The plot, no matter how open-minded y</span>
  <span id="freeText17653090254758085176" style="display:none">This book, this book, this hopelessly stupid book. It's okay. It's something to read. It's not the worst book I've ever read. I did get through the whole thing. But, simply, it is not THAT good.<br>I will now proceed to quote from another reviewer, Mer, who has said exactly what I have been saying for years -albeit she does it far more eloquently than I:<br><br>"The characters are weakly drawn. The dialog is excruciating. The research is shoddy and self-serving at best. The plot, no matter how open-minded you are, is beyond ludicrous.(...)<br>"I'm all for fictional subversion of the dominant Catholic paradigm, but only if the subverter knows what the hell they're talking about. Brown DOESN'T. He's all "la la la, connect the dots" but the picture he comes up with is awkward and unconvincing.<br>"The DaVinci Code is a dead easy, nay, downright lazy read, and yet droves of people are patting themselves on the back for having read and *gasp* actually understood it. Like this is some spectacular achievement? WHY? What, because the slipcover describes it as "erudite"? Are you fucking kidding me?<br>"Don't believe the hype, kids. You are profoundly more intelligent than this holiday page-turner gives you credit for."<br><br>So, so, so true. And if you've read "Angels and Demons" you'll see that it starts out precisely the way "Code" does, nearly word-for-word, even using the dreaded looks-at-himself-in-the-mirror character description cop-out. <br><br>This, and the man (the author, that is) looks like a troll. A self-aggrandizing oh-so-clever stuffed pompous troll. All he did was capitalize on a theme that's been out there for years, insist that it was all 100% factual, and put a pretty red cover on it. He's created a sensation and got himself a movie, I'll give him that. I bet he swims around in vaults of money every night cackling at his deluded readership.<br><br>But the book is just NOT THAT GOOD. Get over it. Want something historical? Read Anya Seton. Something thrilling? Read Thomas Tryon. Richard North Patterson. Jon Krakauer. Croikey, even Clive Cussler! Anything but that damn Dan Brown.</span>

<div id="review_415878287" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/415878287" />
  <a href="/user/show/2679341-jane" class="left imgcol" title="Jane"><img alt="Jane" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1279147542p2/2679341.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/415878287?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Nov 24, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2679341-jane" class="user" title="Jane">Jane</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_2" title="it was ok">2 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/6358141-the-da-vinci-code" class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook ">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/2679341-jane?shelf=audiobook" class="actionLinkLite">audiobook</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/2679341-jane?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/2679341-jane?shelf=on-kindle" class="actionLinkLite">on-kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer415878287" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4992484612888175204">Where I got the book: I downloaded the audiobook on my Audible subscription back when <em>The Da Vinci Code</em>  was a Big Thing, so I suppose I have to admit I technically bought it. A Bad Book Buddy Read with <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/414280464">Crystal Starr Light</a>.<br><br>Plot: The Divine Feminine, mystical messages, the Knights Templar, Opus Dei, secret societies, coded paintings, you name it - Dan Brown packs a Discovery Channel-load of what he gets one of his characters to call "pop schlock" around a puzzle to be solved by granite-jawed Harva</span>
  <span id="freeText4992484612888175204" style="display:none">Where I got the book: I downloaded the audiobook on my Audible subscription back when <em>The Da Vinci Code</em>  was a Big Thing, so I suppose I have to admit I technically bought it. A Bad Book Buddy Read with <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/414280464">Crystal Starr Light</a>.<br><br>Plot: The Divine Feminine, mystical messages, the Knights Templar, Opus Dei, secret societies, coded paintings, you name it - Dan Brown packs a Discovery Channel-load of what he gets one of his characters to call "pop schlock" around a puzzle to be solved by granite-jawed Harvard Professor of Symbology (still makes me laugh every time I think about it) Robert Langdon, a tweedy brainiac, and French police cryptologist Sophie Neveu, who is touted as brilliant but who spends most of the time playing second fiddle to Langdon's encyclopedic knowledge as they run around Europe (in about a 48-hour time period, I believe) being chased by the Evil Catholic Church, the French police, and assorted other baddies. This Europe-wide clue chase has been set in train by Sophie's grandfather who, while being murdered, somehow manages to set up a ridiculously elaborate trail to lead our heroes to the Big Secret.<br><br>Confused? Remember the movie, where Tom Hanks spends 99% of the time with a puzzled frown on his face and the dumbest hairstyle I have EVER seen him wear?<br><br><span>[image error]</span><br><br>That frown is reflected on my face as I try to figure out how in the world this novel got to be so popular. I think the answer lies in the fact that if you just give up on any attempt to analyze what DB is positing, don't worry about the writing and just go with the flow, you get a page-turner that hurtles you toward the point where you are mercifully done with the book. That's what that second star is for. And there are tons of people who love all that esoteric-mystery stuff, plus the book came to the attention of a certain type of Christian who is very easy to bait into perceiving any criticism of any aspect of Christianity as A HUGE THREAT, and the resulting kerfuffle must have had DB's publicist offering up heartfelt prayers of thanksgiving.<br><br><img src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380391447/753848.jpg" class="escapedImg"><br><br>Nope, I'm still puzzled. On my second time around I really paid attention to the writing, and came to the conclusion that <em>The Da Vinci Code</em> is a truly execrable piece of prose that manages to include just about every mistake wannabe novelists are told to avoid. I particularly love the way you're in the middle of an exciting chase-around and then the action suddenly GRINDS TO A HALT while Langdon launches into yet another of his explanations. And the bits where DB was obviously writing with a map of Paris and a guidebook at his elbow, so that you get turn-by-turn street nav and a guided tour of wherever they happen to be, down to the exact dimensions of the room.<br><br>And can you say plot illogicalities? And what about the Moving Body Parts ("Langdon's eyes followed her arm to the structure ahead")? And DB's cringeworthily bad understanding of British, well, everything, as personified in Sir Lee Tebing ('twas an audiobook so the spelling may be wrong, I personally like Surly T-Bing). The other characters, even those who should have known, kept calling him Sir Tebing (it should be Sir Lee) and even, at one glorious moment, "your knightship". And he put clotted cream in his tea... please see <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/user_status/show/19181376">this discussion</a> so I don't have to go over it again. Last but definitely not least, there was supposed to be some sexual attraction between Langdon and Neveu but any time DB went there it was as awkward as watching your brother come on to your BFF. There's something eerily virginal about Langdon which, I swear, manages to communicate itself to Hanks. Never have I seen the Tom look less attractive.<br><br><img src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380391447/753849.jpg" class="escapedImg"><br><br>This is definitely not the best book to listen to as an audiobook unless you are very, very masochistic. The narrator has to do huge chunks of the story in a French accent and then there's Lee Tebing, who got a ludicrously overblown stage British heehaw voice in my version. Then there was the pronunciation of Louvre as LOOV and Tuileries as TOOLERIES but you know, I've got to hand it to this guy - to wade through a reading of this scab on the body literary must be quite the endurance test.<br><br>As a Bad Book read, it's superb. I had to stop about every two minutes in some chapters because there were just so, so many things wrong with this book. See <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/415878287">here</a> for the full roundup. I did, however, fall asleep in a couple of places, as I have done EVERY TIME I have attempted to watch the movie.<br><br><img src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380391447/753850.jpg" class="escapedImg"><br><br>Finding the Hanks images has been about the only thing that's kept me going through this review. The success of this loose stool of a novel remains one of the Great Mysteries of the Age.<br></span>
  <a data-text-id="4992484612888175204" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <a href="/user/show/841949-tracey" class="user" title="Tracey">Tracey</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

                <a href="/review/list/841949-tracey?shelf=no-longer-owned" class="actionLinkLite">no-longer-owned</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer159257545" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer416061253477043410">I didn't realize I never bothered with a review for this; I'm cranky today, so now seems like a good time. <br><br>The only good thing about having read this (probably ten years ago) was that I did not pay for it. The woman I was working for at the time forced me to read it; she had read it and thought it was <em>won</em>derful, and since I had some kind of reputation as a reader she wanted (demanded, actually) my opinion. <br><br>I gave it to her. <br><br>I don't really think that has anything to do with the fact that I didn't</span>
  <span id="freeText416061253477043410" style="display:none">I didn't realize I never bothered with a review for this; I'm cranky today, so now seems like a good time. <br><br>The only good thing about having read this (probably ten years ago) was that I did not pay for it. The woman I was working for at the time forced me to read it; she had read it and thought it was <em>won</em>derful, and since I had some kind of reputation as a reader she wanted (demanded, actually) my opinion. <br><br>I gave it to her. <br><br>I don't really think that has anything to do with the fact that I didn't work for her much longer. <br><br>I really did hate this book. I hated this book before I knew it was cool to hate it. <br><br>The writing was mediocre â that's not why I hated it, though it made (makes) me sick that something that led the best-seller list for approximately a hundred and sixty-three years was so <em>bad</em>. <br><br>I don't generally go for international suspense thriller type things â that's not why I hated it either. <br><br>I was raised Catholic, and this was an absurd view of a conspiracy-laden church that was so dumb as to be offensive â but that â the Vatican's opinion aside â is still  not why I hated it. <br><br>The reason I got that blue-laser-beam duck-and-cover light in my eyes when I read this was that it completely and totally screwed with art history. I kick myself once in a while that I deleted the web page I put up at the time outlining and illustrating the things Dan Brown simply got wrong about the paintings and artists he pretended to know so much about. There was a lot of stuff up there. I'm no expert; I went to art school, though, and the best teacher I ever had in any subject happened to be the one who taught History of Western Art. It offended me on her behalf that there was so much garbage spewed out in this book. <br><br>Someone defensively said to me once that at least it was bringing attention to Da Vinci and art and the Louvre and â¦ stuff. To me that's like saying an A-list celebrity benefits from the attention of paparazzi. There's a strong similarity: if all a person goes by is the sordid and erroneous crap spoon-fed to them by such authorities as Dan Brown or the National Inquirer (Enquirer? Can't be bothered to check), they would be better off remaining completely ignorant.  <br><br>I'm still cranky. Hm â¦ Did I ever review Twilight â¦? </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/11511116-marwa-a?shelf=favorite" class="actionLinkLite">favorite</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/11511116-marwa-a?shelf=thriller-mystery-suspense" class="actionLinkLite">thriller-mystery-suspense</a>,

                <a href="/review/list/11511116-marwa-a?shelf=goodreads-hype" class="actionLinkLite">goodreads-hype</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer382712579" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7940380434613279393"><strong>
  <em>âLife is filled with secrets. You can't learn them all at once.â</em>
</strong><br><br>This is one of the best and most amazing novels I've ever read!<br>When I finished it at almost 3 am, I couldn't sleep.<br>From the instant the book starts, Dan Brown immediately grabs the readers attention, grabbing them by the throat and making them read on right until the end!<br>I like conspiracy theories, so the whole basis of the book was interesting.<br>Obviously most of what is in the book is fiction, but Brown's story telling makes you th</span>
  <span id="freeText7940380434613279393" style="display:none"><strong>
  <em>âLife is filled with secrets. You can't learn them all at once.â</em>
</strong><br><br>This is one of the best and most amazing novels I've ever read!<br>When I finished it at almost 3 am, I couldn't sleep.<br>From the instant the book starts, Dan Brown immediately grabs the readers attention, grabbing them by the throat and making them read on right until the end!<br>I like conspiracy theories, so the whole basis of the book was interesting.<br>Obviously most of what is in the book is fiction, but Brown's story telling makes you think it's real. <br>I thought his use of real places, people and events in what is a fictional story was very clever.<br><br>If you're into mystery/suspense genre, history or secret societies, I recommend this.<br><br>If you are a devout Christian, I don't think I'd recommend this book.</span>

<div id="review_75566740" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75566740" />
  <a href="/user/show/1713956-manny" class="left imgcol" title="Manny"><img alt="Manny" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1227275844p2/1713956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/75566740?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Oct 24, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1713956-manny" class="user" title="Manny">Manny</a>

          marked it as to-read




        

    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer75566740" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4577640785281696796">Fellow Goodreaders, I have a confession to make. (Strikes Abe Lincoln pose). No, I haven't actually read it, if that's what you're thinking. But, in a way, it's worse. The fact is, I... er... I... I'm sorry, this is rather difficult for me... I once, ah, I once wrote a letter to a national newspaper supporting Dan Brown's book. And had it published. <br><br>OK, I've said it, and now I feel better. (Wipes sweat from forehead). I tried to find the offending item just now on Google, but it looks as though</span>
  <span id="freeText4577640785281696796" style="display:none">Fellow Goodreaders, I have a confession to make. (Strikes Abe Lincoln pose). No, I haven't actually read it, if that's what you're thinking. But, in a way, it's worse. The fact is, I... er... I... I'm sorry, this is rather difficult for me... I once, ah, I once wrote a letter to a national newspaper supporting Dan Brown's book. And had it published. <br><br>OK, I've said it, and now I feel better. (Wipes sweat from forehead). I tried to find the offending item just now on Google, but it looks as though well-meaning people have done their best to hide the evidence. I'd really like to thank them for that. Anyway, if you search on my name and "Da Vinci Code" or "Dan Brown", you'll find pointers to it, though I've so far been unable to retrieve the actual text.<br><br>As far as I can recall, the background was roughly as follows. A columnist in the <em>Independent</em>, Christina Patterson, had written an article in which she dismissively attacked <em>The Da Vinci Code</em>, and cited a recent interview with the author. Mr Brown had been asked why he thought his book was a success, and had said something about how he believed that what people liked was books with "puzzles and treasure hunts". Patterson remarked with evident contempt that there were no puzzles and treasure hunts in, if I'm remembering correctly, Shakespeare, Dickens or Tolstoy.<br><br>Well... I'm a big fan of due process. I thought Saddam Hussein was a monster who deserved death fifty times over; but I opposed the Iraq War on the grounds that virtually the only bad thing he hadn't done was to harbor secret weapons of mass destruction, which was the ostensible reason for invading his country. My feelings about Dan Brown were similar. So the letter pointed out that Ms. Patterson was just cherry-picking her authors. As far as I was aware, she was quite correct in saying that Shakespeare, Dickens and Tolstoy didn't do puzzles and treasure-hunts; but if she'd wanted to argue the contrary position, she could just as easily have cited Lewis Carroll, James Joyce and Vladimir Nabokov. The real problem with Dan Brown was not the subject matter, but the quality of the writing.<br><br>Sigh. I thought I'd better come clean. I'd rather that you hear it from me directly than, you know, just stumble over it by accident when you're following a random link. Maybe, some day, you'll be able to forgive me. And while we're talking about Dan Brown and random links, check <a href="http://houseoffame.blogspot.com/2006/05/cipher-of-leonardo.html" rel="nofollow">this</a> out. It's much funnier than my so-called review.<br></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5863240869933423368">it's an action movie! it's a book! it's an action movie!<br><br>it surprises me that the movie version was so dull, having such a simple adapted screenplay to write. this book reads like a blockbuster looks. and i will admit that i totally loved it while i was reading and forgot it promptly after, but i did the same when i watched vin diesel in XXX. <br><br>it's a very fun read. it's horrendously written, the characters are rather shallow, there are enough chase scenes and things popping out of dark corners to</span>
  <span id="freeText5863240869933423368" style="display:none">it's an action movie! it's a book! it's an action movie!<br><br>it surprises me that the movie version was so dull, having such a simple adapted screenplay to write. this book reads like a blockbuster looks. and i will admit that i totally loved it while i was reading and forgot it promptly after, but i did the same when i watched vin diesel in XXX. <br><br>it's a very fun read. it's horrendously written, the characters are rather shallow, there are enough chase scenes and things popping out of dark corners to satisfy anyone's juvenile appetite for suspense. and if you're catholic or knowledgeable at all about religion, it does provide some fodder for thought in between all the drama. but after you think, you realize that none of this information, true or not, is really that shocking and has little effect on the catholic faith. people would do well to learn about the nature and history of their belief system a little more.  <br><br>i still can't figure out how this book has caused so much controversy. let me rephrase that. i still can't figure out how people can be so oblivious and closeminded as to be scandalized by drivelly fiction. i was on an airplane a while ago and i sat next to a man reading "cracking the davinci code" or one of those other pissed off books that came out soon after. i asked him what he thought of the book. he said, 'oh! i've never read the da vinci code! it goes against the catholic church! why would i read such blasphemy? i just want to be armed with information when i speak to some simple-minded person who believes that heretic dan brown!" <br><br>i wish i could make that kind of stuff up. <br><br>i was silent, smiled and nodded, and quickly opened up Kavalier and Clay. i simply had no idea how to respond to someone who was taking notes on a book to collect information on a novel that he had never read so he could disprove the opinions of those who thought it was true. what?<br><br><br></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11958356859553883258">Believe it or not, my BOSS lent me this book. He said it was so great that I HAD to read it, and he pressed it into my hands. I said thanks in surprise, because that was about as personal as my boss ever got.  well, imagine the awkwardness when I didn't really like the book. what do you say to the man who signs your paychecks when you think his book is nothing more than a creative version of what I call the man's romance novel--boring, average man (okay, in this book very smart--but not special l</span>
  <span id="freeText11958356859553883258" style="display:none">Believe it or not, my BOSS lent me this book. He said it was so great that I HAD to read it, and he pressed it into my hands. I said thanks in surprise, because that was about as personal as my boss ever got.  well, imagine the awkwardness when I didn't really like the book. what do you say to the man who signs your paychecks when you think his book is nothing more than a creative version of what I call the man's romance novel--boring, average man (okay, in this book very smart--but not special looking) finds incredibly beautiful and often smart woman to follow him through thick and thin and all manner of ludicrous plots and danger for some unknown reason.  It's just a religious conspiracy version of the Bourne Identity or any other book like that.  What average guy wouldn't love to believe that a hot woman would follow him around, as he impresses and saves her from all kinds of dangers.<br>except I don't get much out of that myself, not being a guy, and I didn't think there was much to the whole plot of religious conspiracy, having majored in Biblical studies in college, and knowing how hokey and unrealistic some of this stuff is. and don't even get me started on weak arguments and faulty logic. (SPOILER ALERT: "don't worship Jesus because He was a normal guy who married a woman and had a kid--no, but go ahead and pray to his fully human wife who's DEAD!" yes, clearly a good argument.) <br>but, hey, my boss likes Jennifer Aniston a lot too, and she doesn't do much for me either. ;)</span>
