<div id="review_26773923" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26773923" />
  <a href="/user/show/972904-jake" class="left imgcol" title="Jake"><img alt="Jake" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1230756051p2/972904.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/26773923?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Jul 09, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/972904-jake" class="user" title="Jake">Jake</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/85266.The_Da_Vinci_Code" class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385504201">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/972904-jake?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/972904-jake?shelf=pulp" class="actionLinkLite">pulp</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26773923" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3475264221558398322">If for some reason you havenât heard of the Da Vinci Code before now, I have no help for you. I live under a fairly substantive rock, and even I had heard of this book to death. I had even attempted to read it once or twice, during my stint at Waldenbooks, but couldnât really get into it. So I gave up, and decided to relegate it to the same place as Titanic and Forrest Gumpâworks of popular culture that I have no interest in, and therefore, will not partake of. But in a testament to the power of</span>
  <span id="freeText3475264221558398322" style="display:none">If for some reason you havenât heard of the Da Vinci Code before now, I have no help for you. I live under a fairly substantive rock, and even I had heard of this book to death. I had even attempted to read it once or twice, during my stint at Waldenbooks, but couldnât really get into it. So I gave up, and decided to relegate it to the same place as Titanic and Forrest Gumpâworks of popular culture that I have no interest in, and therefore, will not partake of. But in a testament to the power of the feminine wile, SV convinced me to read it, and so, at the suggestion of Professor Mortis, borrowed a copy from my local library before setting out for the Thanksgiving holiday (Thanks to PG and Laurion for their lending offers, though I did not take them up on it.).<br><br>So what did I think of this incredibly popular, highly controversial work that sold literally millions of copies, spawned a movie, and produced comments from the Vatican itself? In a word, itâs bad. Other appropriate single words might be awful, atrocious, terrible, or abominable. Other similar synonyms can be found here.<br><br>The Da Vinci Code is a combination of bad writing, shoddy history, poor plotting, and unconvincing characters that blends together to form a barely readable work. The fact that anyone took this work seriously in any capacity is amazing to me. The characters are flat, boring, and none of them exceed two dimensions at best. The âhistoryâ is so riddled with holes as to be completely unbelievable to the semi-alert observer (my favorite part is the explanation of how the Churchâs desire to destroy the âsacred feminineâ is responsible for Orthodox Jews and devout Muslims barring women from certain rites.). And finally, the plot just makes no damn sense. Even the much vaunted puzzles are fairly trite, and usually explained so quickly that itâs barely worth thinking about. The final puzzle that the main character is faced with is so mind-bogglingly obvious that anyone who knows how to draw a Mogen David (Star of David) can figure it out instantly.<br><br>So the question for me became this: given how awful this book is, why the hell is it so popular? I can offer the following explanations/observations.<br><br>First off, Brown employs a clever (and I use the term loosely) narrative structure in which he ends every single chapter on a cliffhanger. This forces the reader to continue reading, hoping to reach a payoff or stopping point that is never quite realized. Thus the reader keeps going to the next chapter, hoping to finally hit a point where the story will relax, only to discover that the story never really relaxes. It just plods relentlessly forward, like the Terminator chasing Sarah Connor with a SuDoKu puzzle.<br><br>In a similar vein, Brown keeps his chapters short. In fact, Iâve read verse poetry that was longer than some of the chapters in this book (and considerably better written). This helps the reader feel as though they are making progress, because theyâre suddenly on Chapter 44, and can reach Chapter 50 with only a few extra minutes of reading. Hooray for the short attention span.<br><br>Likewise, Brown taps into some popular political and historical fallacies that are guaranteed to make him well-liked. Namely that the Catholic Church is an evil conspiracy (Christianity is one of the few religions itâs still ok to hate in this day and age, after all) and the notion that ancient Europeans were all ecologically conscious, goddess-worshipping pacifists until the evil patriarchy destroyed their edenic culture. And hey, both of those are popular, if totally un-nuanced and historically questionable viewpoints, but shoddy history is always popular with the masses. The popularity of this book is proof enough.<br><br>Knowing that Iâm one of the last people in the United States to have read this book, I can hardly imagine my recommendation can save anyone at this point, but on the off hand chance it canâdonât read this book. Itâs a waste of time. At the very least, take it out of the library so you donât put more money into the pocket of an author of questionable talent. Thereâs much better writers in this world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3475264221558398322" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>

<div id="review_75277491" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75277491" />
  <a href="/user/show/2863914-joel" class="left imgcol" title="Joel"><img alt="Joel" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1407175214p2/2863914.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/75277491?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Jun 30, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2863914-joel" class="user" title="Joel">Joel</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/2863914-joel?shelf=to-not-read-ever" class="actionLinkLite">to-not-read-ever</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/2863914-joel?shelf=30-day-book-challenge" class="actionLinkLite">30-day-book-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer75277491" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13776351492957867017">One night about five years back, I stopped over at a friend's house to drop off a movie I'd borrowed. Her husband greeted me at the door and said she would have come out to say hi, but she was too busy reading. "Oh, what's she reading?" I asked. "The Da Vinci Code. She said it's, like, the best book ever."<br><br>I was shocked and appalled. Surely not! Not from my 19th century lit-loving friend, whose main criterion for a good book is that the author is no longer living, and has preferably been that way</span>
  <span id="freeText13776351492957867017" style="display:none">One night about five years back, I stopped over at a friend's house to drop off a movie I'd borrowed. Her husband greeted me at the door and said she would have come out to say hi, but she was too busy reading. "Oh, what's she reading?" I asked. "The Da Vinci Code. She said it's, like, the best book ever."<br><br>I was shocked and appalled. Surely not! Not from my 19th century lit-loving friend, whose main criterion for a good book is that the author is no longer living, and has preferably been that way for at least 100 years. I talked to her on the phone the next day and she explained, to my relief, that she didn't think the book was <em>well written</em> (you know, <em>good</em>), she just thought it was interesting, and appreciated the way it subverted religion and revealed the lengths people will go to to justify and reinforce their beliefs, even in the face of overwhelming evidence to the contrary (she was in a big anti-religion phase at the time).<br><br>"Phew!" I thought. "Friendship crisis averted."<br><br>Then just today at work, conversation turned to popular fiction. It started with Stieg Larsson, and that I am totally on board with -- even though the man doesn't so much write stories as explain to you what is happening in them, in Lisbeth Salander he created a character for the ages (hyperbole alert). I kept my scoffs to myself when co-worker A mentioned that she had <em>looved</em> Water for Elephants, finding it a wonderful break from her usual diet of <em>James Patterson</em>, though my nose may have begun to point ever so slightly upwards (full disclosure: I thought the former a perfectly cromulent, perfectly forgettable plane read, though I can't recall ever reading The World's Best-Selling Author).<br><br>But then.<br><br>Then co-worker B said it: "Have you read The Da Vinci Code? That is seriously the <em>best</em> book ever."<br><br>I could have filled a terabyte hard drive with my sighs and eye rolls. <br><br>Um. Except. Except I have never <em>read</em> The Da Vinci Code, or anything by Dan Brown. Normally I am strongly against literary snobbery (you have to be when you read as many genre books as I have been lately), but everyone has a weak spot, and mine is that book. Can't say why. The hype? The overblown controversy? The terrible movie? Tom Hanks' terrible, terrible mullet?<br><br><img src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380328445/691663.jpg" class="escapedImg"><br><br>The thing is, sometimes I will disparage this book aloud, in conversation, as if I know what I am talking about. Look, I even gave it a star rating. I am such a pretentious jerk sometimes.<br><br>But I can't help feeling that the book deserves it.<br><br><em>Facebook 30 Day Book Challenge Day 23: Book you tell people youâve read, but havenât.</em></span>
  <a data-text-id="13776351492957867017" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_18611979" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18611979" />
  <a href="/user/show/1022982-brad" class="left imgcol" title="Brad"><img alt="Brad" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1308747432p2/1022982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/18611979?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">May 02, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1022982-brad" class="user" title="Brad">Brad</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=most-hated" class="actionLinkLite">most-hated</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18611979" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16654861009045874107">I freely admit that my disdain for <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a> is my own personal backlash over its popularity. <br><br>Dan Brown isn't a terrible writer, despite facing that charge from many experienced readers. He has a likable style, and he drives the pace of the book relentlessly, which is exactly what one would want from a pulpy adventure that one can take to the beach. <br><br>Likewise, the charge that <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a> is somehow a failure because it is in any way inaccurate or unbelievable is unfair. The story is</span>
  <span id="freeText16654861009045874107" style="display:none">I freely admit that my disdain for <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a> is my own personal backlash over its popularity. <br><br>Dan Brown isn't a terrible writer, despite facing that charge from many experienced readers. He has a likable style, and he drives the pace of the book relentlessly, which is exactly what one would want from a pulpy adventure that one can take to the beach. <br><br>Likewise, the charge that <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a> is somehow a failure because it is in any way inaccurate or unbelievable is unfair. The story is <em>fiction</em>, after all, and one should expect to have his/her credulity stretched, especially when reading pulp that is written with the screen in mind (as <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a> surely was).<br><br>I even enjoyed the Sunday afternoon it took me to read <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a>. It was an absolute waste of time and exactly what I wanted to be doing, sitting on a comfy sofa, drinking tea and reading about self-flagellating albino monks (and other fun things).<br><br>I've given many books that are just as good as <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a> and even some that are worse three stars, and I meant every star. The truth is that on its own merits, I'd have given <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a> a similar rating if not for a repeated experience that led to my backlash.<br><br>At the beginning of every semester, in a bid to get to know my students better, I play a memory game wherein the students provide me with their favourite things (books, food, music) and some personal details (people they hate, people they love, things they are proud of), then I connect something about them, something that stands out for me, with their name. It is a good start in getting to know the students, but it has also led to my hatred for Dan Brown's <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a>.<br><br>A good half of the students that enter my courses declare that they don't have favourite books, and/or they've only ever read three books in their lives -- two involuntary (both assigned by an English teacher, and always seeming to include <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/988811.To_Kill_a_Mockingbird" title="To Kill a Mockingbird by Harper Lee">To Kill a Mockingbird</a>) and one voluntary (<a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a>). What pisses me off most is that even if these people liked <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a>, Brown's novel didn't spur them on to read more. They read the <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a>, enjoyed it or didn't, then went back to their reading apathy. <br><br>Moreover, if I could convince people to read one book voluntarily, one book for their pleasure, it would not be ANY cheesy, pulpy, low grade adventure story. It's like pouring a glass of $9 dollar wine for a person who is trying wine for the first time. They may enjoy the glass, but they're not going to choose wine as their alcohol of choice based on Fortant de France. <br><br>And for that reason, I hate <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a>. It is the cheap wine that keeps people away from the joy of good wine, and while I admit that it is the fault of popular culture rather than <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/630.Dan_Brown" title="Dan Brown">Dan Brown</a>, each reader I find who stops at <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a> makes me hate the book a little bit more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16654861009045874107" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3270603368214299969">I read <em>Da Vinci Code</em> and enjoyed it, but it was a mildly guilty pleasure. The analogy with junk food is mostly appropriate, at least for readers. (No</span>
  <span id="freeText3270603368214299969" style="display:none">I read <em>Da Vinci Code</em> and enjoyed it, but it was a mildly guilty pleasure. The analogy with junk food is mostly appropriate, at least for readers. (No one <em>must</em> read, but it works for those of us that choose to read.)<br><br>Every hour I spend reading fluff is an hour not spent reading something I am somewhat sure is "better for me" in some way. But what I think is better for me has no certain relationship to anyone else's self-improvement. <br><br>That said, it does grate when someone does a mediocre job and is lauded with wealth and popular acclaim. Dan Brown isn't bad, but there are plenty of authors I'd rather see receiving bucketfuls of money. I'm much happier with the success of J.K. Rowling, for example.<br><br><br>And Trevorâ please do read both <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/119073.The_Name_of_the_Rose" title="The Name of the Rose by Umberto Eco">The Name of the Rose</a> and <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a>. The former for yourself (it is a <em>splendid</em> novel), and the latter so we can all greedily consume your review.<br><br>Hmmm, perhaps we should start a series of polls wherein we can vote on what Trevor should read and review next?</span>

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
  document.onclick = checkOpenSelects;

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('f4g74yQCWmOoqH6EoKj29brdwFPkdMyhdyUyGpL8plnfQFUXRQ9tGaoYQSxbqZmV3Zc8+HoGBt3jueHf92Puxw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jshu7nklwur9ZDloTm0oQVWopq4aULt0SWnugqdbQ5EuAAAaGCj1kP/UBsC1bEchMuJaBYQicQjd9T1HwsQLDw==')})
  }
</script>

    <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/630.Dan_Brown">About Dan Brown</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/630.Dan_Brown"><img alt="630" class="right" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1399396714p2/630.jpg" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer17953241364034290000">Dan Brown is the author of numerous #1 bestselling novels, including <em>The Da Vinci Code</em>, which has become one of the best selling novels of all time as well as the subject of intellectual debate among readers and scholars. Brownâs novels are published in 52 languages around the world with 200 million copies in print.<br><br>In 2005, Brown was named one of the 100 Most Influential People in the World by TIM</span>
  <span id="freeText17953241364034290000" style="display:none">Dan Brown is the author of numerous #1 bestselling novels, including <em>The Da Vinci Code</em>, which has become one of the best selling novels of all time as well as the subject of intellectual debate among readers and scholars. Brownâs novels are published in 52 languages around the world with 200 million copies in print.<br><br>In 2005, Brown was named one of the 100 Most Influential People in the World by TIME Magazine, whose editors credited him with âkeeping the publishing industry afloat; renewed interest in Leonardo da Vinci and early Christian history; spiking tourism to Paris and Rome; a growing membership in secret societies; the ire of Cardinals in Rome; eight books denying the claims of the novel and seven guides to read along with it; a flood of historical thrillers; and a major motion picture franchise.â<br><br>The son of a mathematics teacher and a church organist, Brown was raised on a prep school campus where he developed a fascination with the paradoxical interplay between science and religion. These themes eventually formed the backdrop for his books. He is a graduate of Amherst College and Phillips Exeter Academy, where he later returned to teach English before focusing his attention full time to writing.<br><br>Brown is currently at work on a new book as well as the Columbia Pictures film version of his most recent novel.<br></span>
